<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading">Crusades</h1>

<p><b><a href="/wiki/Popular_crusades" title="Popular crusades">Popular crusades</a></b>
</p>
<b><a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> (718–1492)</b></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p>The <b>Crusades</b> were a series of religious wars initiated, supported, and sometimes directed by the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_period" title="Medieval period">medieval period</a>. The best known of these Crusades are those to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> in the period between 1095 and 1291 that were intended to recover <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Jerusalem and its surrounding</a> area from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Islamic rule</a>. Concurrent military activities in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a></i>) and in northern Europe against pagan <a href="/wiki/West_Slavs" title="West Slavs">West Slavic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balts" title="Balts">Baltic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltic_Finns" title="Baltic Finns">Finnic</a> peoples (the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a>) also became known as crusades. Through the 15th century, other church-sanctioned crusades were fought against heretical Christian sects, against the Byzantine and Ottoman empires, to combat paganism and heresy, and for political reasons. Unsanctioned by the church, <a href="/wiki/Popular_crusades" title="Popular crusades">Popular Crusades</a> of ordinary citizens were also frequent. Beginning with the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> which resulted in the recovery of Jerusalem in 1099, dozens of Crusades were fought, providing a focal point of European history for centuries.
</p><p>In 1095, Pope <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Urban II</a> proclaimed the First Crusade at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Clermont" title="Council of Clermont">Council of Clermont</a>. He encouraged military support for <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">Byzantine emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios<span class="nowrap"> </span>I</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuk Turks</a> and called for an armed pilgrimage to Jerusalem. Across all social strata in western Europe, there was an enthusiastic popular response. The first Crusaders had a variety of motivations, including religious salvation, satisfying feudal obligations, opportunities for renown, and economic or political advantage. Later crusades were generally conducted by more organized armies, sometimes led by a king. All were granted papal <a href="/wiki/Indulgence#Early_and_medieval_beliefs" title="Indulgence">indulgences</a>. Initial successes established four <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">Crusader states</a>: the <a href="/wiki/County_of_Edessa" title="County of Edessa">County of Edessa</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Antioch" title="Principality of Antioch">Principality of Antioch</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/County_of_Tripoli" title="County of Tripoli">County of Tripoli</a>. The Crusader presence remained in the region in some form until the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Acre_(1291)" title="Siege of Acre (1291)">fall of Acre</a> in 1291. After this, there were no further crusades to recover the Holy Land.
</p><p>Proclaimed a crusade in 1123, the struggle between the <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> was called the <i><a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a></i> by Christians, and only ended in 1492 with the fall of the Muslim <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a>. From 1147, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">campaigns</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a> against pagan tribes were considered crusades. In 1199, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Pope Innocent III</a> began the practice of proclaiming political crusades against Christian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heretics" title="Heretics">heretics</a>. In the 13th century, crusading was used against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathars" title="Cathars">Cathars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Languedoc" title="Languedoc">Languedoc</a> and against <a href="/wiki/Banate_of_Bosnia" title="Banate of Bosnia">Bosnia</a>; this practice continued against the <a href="/wiki/Waldensians" title="Waldensians">Waldensians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Savoy" title="Savoy">Savoy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussites</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a> in the 15th<span class="nowrap"> </span>century and against <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestants</a> in the 16th. From the mid-14th<span class="nowrap"> </span>century, crusading rhetoric was used in response to the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, only ending in 1699 with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_League_(1684)" title="Holy League (1684)">War of the Holy League</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Terminology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Terminology</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Crusades_and_the_Holy_Land,_1095–1291"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Crusades and the Holy Land, 1095–1291</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Combat_deuxi%C3%A8me_croisade.jpg"><img alt="Medieval illustration of a battle during the Second Crusade" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Combat_deuxi%C3%A8me_croisade.jpg/300px-Combat_deuxi%C3%A8me_croisade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Combat_deuxi%C3%A8me_croisade.jpg/450px-Combat_deuxi%C3%A8me_croisade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Combat_deuxi%C3%A8me_croisade.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Combat_deuxi%C3%A8me_croisade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>14th-century miniature of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">Second Crusade</a> battle from the <i><a href="/wiki/Estoire_d%27Eracles" title="Estoire d'Eracles">Estoire d'Eracles</a></i></div></div></div><p>The term "crusade" first referred to military expeditions undertaken by European Christians in the 11th, 12th, and 13th<span class="nowrap"> </span>centuries to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>. The conflicts to which the term is applied has been extended to include other campaigns initiated, supported and sometimes directed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> against <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heresy_in_Christianity" title="Heresy in Christianity">heretics</a> or for alleged religious ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyerman20091–12Chapter_1:_Definition_[of_the_Crusades]_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyerman20091–12Chapter_1:_Definition_[of_the_Crusades]-1">[1]</a></sup> These differed from other Christian religious wars in that they were considered a penitential exercise, and so earned participants forgiveness for all confessed sins. The term's usage can be misleading, particularly regarding the early Crusades, and the definition remains a matter of debate among contemporary historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the time of the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">iter</i></span>, "journey", and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">peregrinatio</i></span>, "pilgrimage" were used to describe the campaign. Crusader terminology remained largely indistinguishable from that of Christian pilgrimage during the 12th<span class="nowrap"> </span>century. Only at the end of the century was a specific language of Crusading adopted in the form of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">crucesignatus</i></span>—"one signed by the cross"—for a Crusader. This led to the French <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">croisade</i></span>—the way of the cross.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:103_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:103-3">[3]</a></sup> By the mid 13th<span class="nowrap"> </span>century the cross became the major descriptor of the Crusades with <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">crux transmarina</i></span>—"the cross overseas"—used for crusades in the eastern Mediterranean, and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">crux cismarina</i></span>—"the cross this side of the sea"—for those in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyerman2006894The_Later_Crusades_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyerman2006894The_Later_Crusades-4">[4]</a></sup> The modern English "crusade" dates to the 17th century, with the work of <a href="/wiki/Louis_Maimbourg" title="Louis Maimbourg">Louis Malmbourg</a>.<i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></i> Strategic raiding was known as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passagium_particulare" title="Passagium particulare">passagium particulare</a></i></span> and more fundamental campaigns as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">passagium generale</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHousley1995260_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHousley1995260-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The terms "Franks" (<i>Franj</i>) and "Latins" were used by the peoples of the Near East during the crusades for western Europeans, distinguishing them from the Byzantine Christians who were known as "Greeks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saracen" title="Saracen">Saracen</a> was used for an Arab Muslim, derived from a Greek and Roman name for the nomadic peoples of the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Desert" title="Syrian Desert">Syro-Arabian desert</a>. Crusader sources used the term "Syrians" to describe Arabic speaking Christians who were members of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek Orthodox Church</a>, and "Jacobites" for those who were members of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syrian Orthodox Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The Crusader states of Syria and Palestine were known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outremer" title="Outremer">Outremer</a>" from the French <i>outre-mer</i>, or "the land beyond the sea".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Crusades_and_the_Holy_Land.2C_1095.E2.80.931291"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crusades_and_the_Holy_Land,_1095–1291">Crusades and the Holy Land, 1095–1291</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Timeline of the Kingdom of Jerusalem">Timeline of the Kingdom of Jerusalem</a></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre-Jerusalem.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre-Jerusalem.JPG/300px-The_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre-Jerusalem.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre-Jerusalem.JPG/450px-The_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre-Jerusalem.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/The_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre-Jerusalem.JPG/600px-The_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre-Jerusalem.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre-Jerusalem.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Church of the Holy Sepulchre</a> in Jerusalem</div></div></div>
<p>The Crusades to the Holy Land are the best known of the religious wars discussed here, beginning in 1095 and lasting some two centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> These Crusades began with the fervent desire to liberate the Holy Land from the Muslims, and ran through eight major numbered crusades and dozens of minor crusades over two centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristie2014Chronology_of_the_Crusades_(1055–1291)_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristie2014Chronology_of_the_Crusades_(1055–1291)-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arab%E2%80%93Byzantine_wars" title="Arab–Byzantine wars">Arab-Byzantine wars</a>, begun in the 7th century, were essentially over by 995, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">Byzantine emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Basil_II" title="Basil II">Basil II</a> was able to extend the empire's territorial recovery to its furthest extent in 1025, with frontiers stretching east to Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> It controlled Bulgaria, much of southern Italy and suppressed piracy in the Mediterranean Sea. The empire's relationships with its Islamic neighbours were no more quarrelsome than its relationships with the <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a> and the Western Christians. The <a href="/wiki/Italo-Normans" title="Italo-Normans">Normans</a> in Italy,  to the north <a href="/wiki/Pechenegs" title="Pechenegs">Pechenegs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cumans" title="Cumans">Cumans</a>, and Seljuk Turks in the east all competed with the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJotischky200441–43_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJotischky200441–43-14">[14]</a></sup> The political situation in the Middle East was changed by waves of Turkish migration—in particular, the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_dynasty" title="Seljuk dynasty">Seljuk Turks</a> in the 10th<span class="nowrap"> </span>century. Previously a minor ruling clan from Transoxania, they were recent converts to Islam who migrated into Iran to seek their fortune. In two decades, they conquered Iran, Iraq, and the Near East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Byzantium's attempted confrontation in 1071 to suppress the Seljuks' sporadic raiding led to the defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">battle of Manzikert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOman1924218–228Chapter_IV:_Decline_of_the_Byzantine_Empire_(1071–1204)_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOman1924218–228Chapter_IV:_Decline_of_the_Byzantine_Empire_(1071–1204)-16">[16]</a></sup> In the same year, Jerusalem was taken from the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimids</a> by the Turkish warlord <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atsiz_ibn_Uvaq" title="Atsiz ibn Uvaq">Atsiz</a>, who seized most of Syria and Palestine as part of the expansion of the Seljuks throughout the Middle East. The Seljuk hold on the city resulting in pilgrims reported difficulties and the oppression of Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge201226–29Islam_and_Christian_Europe_on_the_eve_of_the_Crusades_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge201226–29Islam_and_Christian_Europe_on_the_eve_of_the_Crusades-17">[17]</a></sup> The result was the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Crusade">First Crusade</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a></div>
<p>In 1074, just three years after Manzikert and the Seljuk takeover of Jerusalem, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_VII" title="Pope Gregory VII">Gregory VII</a> began planning to launch a military campaign for the liberation of the Holy Land. Twenty years later, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Urban II</a> realized that dream, hosting the decisive <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Piacenza" title="Council of Piacenza">Council of Piacenza</a> and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Clermont" title="Council of Clermont">Council of Clermont</a> in November 1095, that resulted in the mobilization of Western Europe to go to the Holy Land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Byzantine emperor <a href="/wiki/Alexios_I_Komnenos" title="Alexios I Komnenos">Alexios I Komnenos</a>, worried about the continued advances of the Seljuks, sent envoys to these councils asking Urban for aid against the invading Turks. Urban talked of the violence of Europe and the necessity of maintaining the Peace of God; about helping Byzantium; about the crimes being committed against Christians in the east; and about a new kind of war, an armed pilgrimage, and of rewards in heaven, where remission of sins was offered to any who might die in the undertaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The enthusiastic crowd responded with cries of <i><a href="/wiki/Deus_vult" title="Deus vult">Deus lo volt</a>!</i>—God wills it!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyerman200665Summons_to_Jerusalem_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyerman200665Summons_to_Jerusalem-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Peter_the_Hermit.jpg"><img alt="14th-century miniature of Peter the Hermit leading the People's Crusade" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1008" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Peter_the_Hermit.jpg/300px-Peter_the_Hermit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Peter_the_Hermit.jpg/450px-Peter_the_Hermit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Peter_the_Hermit.jpg/600px-Peter_the_Hermit.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Peter_the_Hermit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miniature of <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Hermit" title="Peter the Hermit">Peter the Hermit</a> leading the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Crusade" title="People's Crusade">People's Crusade</a> (<a href="/wiki/Egerton_Collection" title="Egerton Collection">Egerton</a> 1500, Avignon, 14th<span class="nowrap"> </span>century)</div></div></div><p>Immediately after Urban's proclamation, the French priest <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Hermit" title="Peter the Hermit">Peter the Hermit</a> led thousands of mostly poor Christians out of Europe in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Crusade" title="People's Crusade">People's Crusade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In transit through Germany, these Crusaders spawned German bands who massacred Jewish communities in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland_massacres" title="Rhineland massacres">Rhineland massacres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1951134–141The_German_Crusade_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1951134–141The_German_Crusade-22">[22]</a></sup> They were destroyed in 1096 when the main body of Crusaders was annihilated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Civetot" title="Battle of Civetot">battle of Civetot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1951121–133The_People's_Expedition_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1951121–133The_People's_Expedition-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In response to Urban's call, members of the high aristocracy from Europe took the cross. Foremost amongst these was the elder statesman <a href="/wiki/Raymond_IV,_Count_of_Toulouse" title="Raymond IV, Count of Toulouse">Raymond IV of Toulouse</a>, who with bishop <a href="/wiki/Adhemar_of_Le_Puy" title="Adhemar of Le Puy">Adhemar of Le Puy</a> commanded southern French forces. Other armies included one led by <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_of_Bouillon" title="Godfrey of Bouillon">Godfrey of Bouillon</a> and his brother <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_I_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin I of Jerusalem">Baldwin of Boulogne</a>; forces led by <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_I_of_Antioch" title="Bohemond I of Antioch">Bohemond of Taranto</a> and his nephew <a href="/wiki/Tancred,_Prince_of_Galilee" title="Tancred, Prince of Galilee">Tancred</a>; and contingents under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert Curthose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_Count_of_Blois" title="Stephen, Count of Blois">Stephen of Blois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh,_Count_of_Vermandois" title="Hugh, Count of Vermandois">Hugh of Vermandois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_II,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Robert II, Count of Flanders">Robert II of Flanders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge200489–95The_Main_Armies_of_the_First_Crusade_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge200489–95The_Main_Armies_of_the_First_Crusade-24">[24]</a></sup> The armies travelled to Byzantium where they were cautiously welcomed by the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1951336–341Appendix_II:_The_Numerical_Strength_of_the_Crusaders_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1951336–341Appendix_II:_The_Numerical_Strength_of_the_Crusaders-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexios persuaded many of the princes to pledge allegiance to him. He also convinced them their first objective should be Nicaea,  Buoyed by their success at Civetot, the over-confident Seljuks left the city unprotected, thus enabling its capture after the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Nicaea" title="Siege of Nicaea">siege of Nicaea</a> in May–June 1097.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2004117–131The_First_Storm_of_War_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2004117–131The_First_Storm_of_War-26">[26]</a></sup> The first experience of Turkish tactics occurred when a force led by Bohemond and Robert was ambushed at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dorylaeum_(1097)" title="Battle of Dorylaeum (1097)">battle of Dorylaeum</a> in July 1097. The Normans resisted for hours before the arrival of the main army caused a Turkish withdrawal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOman1924273–278Battle_of_Dorylaeum_(1097)_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOman1924273–278Battle_of_Dorylaeum_(1097)-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Crusader army marched to the former Byzantine city of <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> that had been in Muslim control since 1084. The Crusaders began the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antioch" title="Siege of Antioch">siege of Antioch</a> in October 1097 and fought for eight months to a stalemate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Finally, Bohemond persuaded a guard in the city to open a gate. The Crusaders entered, massacring the Muslim inhabitants as well as many Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobson1855368–371Fourth_siege_of_Edessa,_A.D._1097_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobson1855368–371Fourth_siege_of_Edessa,_A.D._1097-29">[29]</a></sup> A force to recapture the city was raised by <a href="/wiki/Kerbogha" title="Kerbogha">Kerbogha</a>, the Seljuk <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Mosul#Seljuk_Atabegs" title="List of rulers of Mosul">atabeg of Mosul</a>.
</p><p>The discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Lance#Antioch" title="Holy Lance">Holy Lance</a> by mystic <a href="/wiki/Peter_Bartholomew" title="Peter Bartholomew">Peter Bartholomew</a> may have boosted the morale of the Crusaders. The Byzantines did not march to the assistance of the Crusaders. Instead Alexius retreated from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philomelium" title="Philomelium">Philomelium</a>. The Greeks were never truly forgiven for this perceived betrayal. The Crusaders attempted to negotiate surrender but were rejected. Bohemond recognised that the only remaining option was open combat and launched a counterattack. Despite superior numbers, the Muslims retreated and abandoned the siege.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2004153–187Before_the_Walls_of_Antioch_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2004153–187Before_the_Walls_of_Antioch-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Proceeding down the Mediterranean coast, the Crusaders encountered little resistance, as local rulers preferred to make peace with them and furnish them with supplies rather than fight. The next significant combat was the <a href="/wiki/Arqa#Crusades_period" title="Arqa">siege of Arqa</a> begun on 14 February 1099. Since the death of Adhemar after Antioch, there had been no spiritual leader of the crusade, and ever since the discovery of the Holy Lance, there had been accusations of fraud among the clerical factions. On 8 April 1099, <a href="/wiki/Arnulf_of_Chocques" title="Arnulf of Chocques">Arnulf of Chocques</a>, chaplain to Robert Curthose, challenged Bartholomew to an ordeal by fire. Peter underwent the ordeal and died after days of agony from his wounds, which discredited the Holy Lance as a fake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The siege of Arqa lasted until 13 May, when the Crusaders left having captured nothing, moving onward towards Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>News arrived that the Fatimids had taken Jerusalem from the Seljuks, making it imperative to attack. Bohemond remained in Antioch, retaining the city, despite his pledge to return it to Byzantine control, while Raymond led the remaining Crusader army rapidly south along the coast to Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1951265–278The_Road_to_Jerusalem_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1951265–278The_Road_to_Jerusalem-33">[33]</a></sup> On 7 June 1099, the Crusaders reached Jerusalem. Many Crusaders wept upon seeing the city they had journeyed so long to reach. An initial attack on the city failed, and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(1099)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (1099)">siege of Jerusalem</a> of 1099 became a stalemate, until they breached the walls on 15 July 1099. <a href="/wiki/Iftikhar_al-Dawla" title="Iftikhar al-Dawla">Iftikhar al-Dawla</a>, the commander of the garrison, struck a deal with Raymond, surrendering the citadel in return for being granted safe passage to <a href="/wiki/Ashkelon" title="Ashkelon">Ascalon</a>. For two days the Crusaders massacred the inhabitants and pillaged the city. Jerusalem had been returned to Christian rule. Urban II died on 29 July 1099, fourteen days after the fall of Jerusalem to the Crusaders, but before news of the event had reached Italy. He was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paschal_II" title="Pope Paschal II">Paschal II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>
On July 22, 1099, a council was held in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Church of the Holy Sepulchre</a> and Godfrey of Bouillon took the leadership, not called king but rather with the title <i>Advocatus Sancti Sepulchri</i> (Defender of the Holy Sepulchre).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1951289–314Advocatus_Sancti_Sepulchri_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1951289–314Advocatus_Sancti_Sepulchri-35">[35]</a></sup> At this point, most Crusaders considered their pilgrimage complete and returned to Europe. Godfrey was left with a small force—a mere 300 knights and 2,000 infantry—to defend the kingdom. The Frankish position was enhanced by defeating an Egyptian relief force at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ascalon" title="Battle of Ascalon">battle of Ascalon</a> in August 1099. The First Crusade thus ended successfully and resulted in the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2004232–327The_Last_Battle_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2004232–327The_Last_Battle-36">[36]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Crusader_States_in_1135.svg"><img alt="map of the Crusader States (1135)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1322" data-file-width="966" decoding="async" height="383" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/The_Crusader_States_in_1135.svg/280px-The_Crusader_States_in_1135.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/The_Crusader_States_in_1135.svg/420px-The_Crusader_States_in_1135.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/The_Crusader_States_in_1135.svg/560px-The_Crusader_States_in_1135.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Crusader_States_in_1135.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Crusader States in 1135</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="The_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem.2C_1099.E2.80.931147"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Kingdom_of_Jerusalem,_1099–1147">The Kingdom of Jerusalem, 1099–1147</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Godfrey_of_Bouillon#Death" title="Godfrey of Bouillon">Godfrey of Bouillon</a> died on 18 July 1100, likely from typhoid. The news of his death was met with mourning in Jerusalem. He was lying in state for five days, before his burial at the Church of the Holy Sepulchre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2012116–118Protector_of_the_Holy_City_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2012116–118Protector_of_the_Holy_City-37">[37]</a></sup> The Jerusalem knights offered the kingdom to Godfrey's brother <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_I_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin I of Jerusalem">Baldwin I of Jerusalem</a>, then Count of Edessa. Godfrey's last battle, the <a href="/wiki/First_siege_of_Arsuf" title="First siege of Arsuf">siege of Arsuf</a>, would be completed by Baldwin in April 1101. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Dagobert_of_Pisa" title="Dagobert of Pisa">Dagobert of Pisa</a>, now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Patriarch_of_Jerusalem" title="Latin Patriarch of Jerusalem">Latin Patriarch of Jerusalem</a>, made the same offer to Bohemond, and asking that he prevent Baldwin's expected travel to Jerusalem. But the letter was intercepted and Bohemond was captured with <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Salerno" title="Richard of Salerno">Richard of Salerno</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Danishmendids" title="Danishmendids">Danishmends</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Melitene" title="Battle of Melitene">battle of Melitene</a> in August 1100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Baldwin I was crowned as the first king of Jerusalem on Christmas Day 1100 by Dagobert at the Church of the Nativity. Baldwin's cousin <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_II_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin II of Jerusalem">Baldwin of Bourcq</a>, later his successor as Baldwin II, was named Count of Edessa, and Tancred became regent of Antioch during Bohemond's captivity, lasting through 1103.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdgington201976–92King_of_Jerusalem_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdgington201976–92King_of_Jerusalem-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crusade_of_1101">Crusade of 1101</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Crusade_of_1101" title="Crusade of 1101">Crusade of 1101</a> was initiated by Paschal II when he learned of the precarious position of the remaining forces in the Holy Land. The host consisted of four separate armies, sometimes regarded as a second wave following the First Crusade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The first army was Lombardy, led by <a href="/wiki/Anselm_IV_(archbishop_of_Milan)" title="Anselm IV (archbishop of Milan)">Anselm, archbishop of Milan</a>. They were joined by a force led by Conrad, constable to the German emperor, <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a>. A second army, the Nivernois, was commanded by <a href="/wiki/William_II,_Count_of_Nevers" title="William II, Count of Nevers">William II of Nevers</a>. The third group from northern France was led by <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_Count_of_Blois" title="Stephen, Count of Blois">Stephen of Blois</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_I,_Count_of_Burgundy" title="Stephen I, Count of Burgundy">Stephen of Burgundy</a>. They were joined by <a href="/wiki/Raymond_IV,_Count_of_Toulouse" title="Raymond IV, Count of Toulouse">Raymond of Saint-Gilles</a>, now in the service of the emperor. The fourth army was led by <a href="/wiki/William_IX,_Duke_of_Aquitaine" title="William IX, Duke of Aquitaine">William IX of Aquitaine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welf_II,_Duke_of_Bavaria" title="Welf II, Duke of Bavaria">Welf IV of Bavaria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArcher1904104–107A_Disastrous_Expedition_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArcher1904104–107A_Disastrous_Expedition-41">[41]</a></sup> The Crusaders faced their old enemy Kilij Arslan and his Seljuk forces first met the Lombard and French contingents in August 1101 at the <a href="/wiki/Crusade_of_1101#Battle_of_Mersivan" title="Crusade of 1101">Battle of Mersivan</a>, with the crusader camp captured. The Nivernois contingent was decimated that same month at <a href="/wiki/Heraclea_Cybistra" title="Heraclea Cybistra">Heraclea</a>, with nearly the entire force wiped out, except for the count William and a few of his men. The Aquitainians and Bavarians reached Heraclea in September where again the Crusaders were massacred. The Crusade of 1101 was a total disaster both militarily and politically, showing the Muslims that the Crusaders were not invincible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_the_kingdom">Establishment of the kingdom</span></h4>
<p>The reign of Baldwin I began in 1100 and oversaw the consolidation of the kingdom in the face of enemies to the north, the Seljuks, and the Fatimids to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHagenmeyer1911ROL,_Tomes_IX,_X,_XI_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHagenmeyer1911ROL,_Tomes_IX,_X,_XI-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Afdal_Shahanshah" title="Al-Afdal Shahanshah">Al-Afdal Shahanshah</a>, the powerful Fatimid vizier, anxious to recover the lands lost to the Franks, initiated the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ramla_(1101)" title="Battle of Ramla (1101)">First Battle of Ramla</a> on 7 September 1101 in which his forces were narrowly defeated, by those of Baldwin I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> On 17 May 1102, the Crusaders were not so lucky, suffering a major defeat at the hands of the Fatimids, under the command of al-Afdal's son Sharaf al-Ma'ali at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ramla_(1102)" title="Battle of Ramla (1102)">Second Battle of Ramla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Among the slain were veterans of the Crusade of 1101, Stephen of Blois and Stephen of Burgundy. Conrad of Germany fought so valiantly that his attackers offered to spare his life if he surrendered. The kingdom was on the verge of collapse after the defeat, recovering after the successful <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ramla_(1102)#Siege_of_Jaffa_and_Aftermath" title="Battle of Ramla (1102)">Battle of Jaffa</a> on 27 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In the north, the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tripoli" title="Siege of Tripoli">Siege of Tripoli</a> was begun, not to be resolved for seven years. Al-Afdal tried once more in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ramla_(1105)" title="Battle of Ramla (1105)">Third Battle of Ramla</a> in August 1105 and was defeated. After the Crusader victory at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Beirut_(1110)" title="Siege of Beirut (1110)">Siege of Beirut</a> in 1110, the Fatimid threat to the kingdom subsided for two decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Harran" title="Battle of Harran">Battle of Harran</a> was fought in 1104, pitting the Crusader states of <a href="/wiki/County_of_Edessa" title="County of Edessa">Edessa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Antioch" title="Principality of Antioch">Antioch</a> against <a href="/wiki/Jikirmish" title="Jikirmish">Jikirmish</a>, who had replaced <a href="/wiki/Kerbogha" title="Kerbogha">Kerbogha</a> as <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Mosul#Seljuk_Atabegs" title="List of rulers of Mosul">atabeg of Mosul</a>, and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B6kmen_(Artuqid)" title="Sökmen (Artuqid)">Sökmen</a>, commander of the Seljuk forces. The ensuing Seljuk victory also resulted in the capture of <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_II_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin II of Jerusalem">Baldwin of Bourcq</a>, then count of Edessa and later king of Jerusalem, and his cousin <a href="/wiki/Joscelin_I,_Count_of_Edessa" title="Joscelin I, Count of Edessa">Joscelin of Courtenay</a>. A Turkish adventurer <a href="/wiki/Jawali_Saqawa" title="Jawali Saqawa">Jawali Saqawa</a> killed Jikirmish in 1106, seizing Mosul and his hostage Baldwin. Separately freed, Joscelin began negotiations with Jawali for Baldwin's release. Expelled from Mosul by <a href="/wiki/Mawdud" title="Mawdud">Mawdud</a>, Jawali fled with his hostage to the fortress of <a href="/wiki/Qal%27at_Ja%27bar" title="Qal'at Ja'bar">Qal'at Ja'bar</a>. Jawali, in need of allies against Mawdud, accepted Joscelin's offer, releasing Baldwin in the summer of 1108.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStevenson190769–120Early_History_of_Antioch_and_Edessa:_Moslem_Reaction_down_to_AD_1127_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStevenson190769–120Early_History_of_Antioch_and_Edessa:_Moslem_Reaction_down_to_AD_1127-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Bohemond was ransomed in 1103, he had resumed control of Antioch and continued the conflict with the Byzantine empire. The Byzantines had taken advantage of Bohemond's absence, retaking lands lost. Bohemond returned to Italy on late 1104 to recruit allies and gather supplies. Tancred again assumed leadership in Antioch, successfully defeating the Seljuks at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Artah" title="Battle of Artah">Battle of Artah</a> in 1105, threatening Aleppo. In the meantime, his uncle began what is known as <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_I_of_Antioch#Wars_between_Antioch_and_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Bohemond I of Antioch">Bohemond's Crusade</a> (or the Crusade of 1107–1108).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYewdale1917115–134Chapter_VIII:_The_Crusade_of_1107_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYewdale1917115–134Chapter_VIII:_The_Crusade_of_1107-49">[49]</a></sup> Bohemond crossed into the Balkans and began the failed <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Dyrrhachium_(1107%E2%80%931108)" title="Siege of Dyrrhachium (1107–1108)">Siege of Dyrrhachium</a>. The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Devol" title="Treaty of Devol">Treaty of Devol</a> of 1108 forced Bohemond to become vassal to the emperor, restore taken lands and other onerous terms. Bohemond never returned. He died in 1111, leaving Tancred as regent to his son <a href="/wiki/Bohemond_II_of_Antioch" title="Bohemond II of Antioch">Bohemond II</a>, who ignored the treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Crusade" title="Norwegian Crusade">Norwegian Crusade</a> also known as the Crusade of <a href="/wiki/Sigurd_the_Crusader" title="Sigurd the Crusader">Sigurd Jorsalfar</a>, king of Norway, took place from 1107 to 1110. More of a pilgrimage than a crusade, it did include the participation in military action at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sidon" title="Siege of Sidon">Siege of Sidon</a> of 1110. Baldwin's army besieged the city by land, while the Norwegians came by sea, and the victorious Crusaders gave similar terms of surrender as given to previous victories at Arsuf in 1102 and at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Acre_(1104)" title="Siege of Acre (1104)">Siege of Acre</a> of 1100–1104, freeing the major port of the kingdom. This Crusade marked the first time a European king visited the Holy Land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in 1110, the Seljuks launched a series of attacks on the Crusader states, in particular Edessa, led by Mawdud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> These included the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shaizar" title="Battle of Shaizar">Battle of Shaizar</a> in 1111, a stalemate. At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_al-Sannabra" title="Battle of al-Sannabra">Battle of al-Sannabra</a> of 1113, a Crusader army led by Baldwin I was defeated by a Muslim army led by Mawdud and <a href="/wiki/Toghtekin" title="Toghtekin">Toghtekin</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Damascus#Burid_emirs" title="List of rulers of Damascus">atabeg of Damascus</a>, whose ultimate objective was Edessa. Mawdud was unable to annihilate the Crusader forces and was soon murdered by <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Assassins" title="Order of Assassins">Assassins</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bursuq_II" title="Bursuq II">Bursuq ibn Bursuq</a> took command of the failed attempt against Edessa in 1114. Finally, <a href="/wiki/Roger_of_Salerno" title="Roger of Salerno">Roger of Salerno</a> routed the last Seljuk invading army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sarmin" title="Battle of Sarmin">First Battle of Tell Danith</a> on 14 September 1115.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2000124–125Relations_with_other_Latin_Settlements_in_the_East_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2000124–125Relations_with_other_Latin_Settlements_in_the_East-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Baldwin I died on 2 April 1118 after an attack on the city of <a href="/wiki/Pelusium" title="Pelusium">Pelusium</a> on the Nile. He was buried in Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_II_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin II of Jerusalem">Baldwin II of Jerusalem</a> became king on 14 April 1118, but was there was not a formal coronation until Christmas Day 1119 due to issues concerning his wife <a href="/wiki/Morphia_of_Melitene" title="Morphia of Melitene">Morphia of Melitene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The early days of Baldwin II's reign included the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ager_Sanguinis" title="Battle of Ager Sanguinis">Battle of Ager Sanguinis</a>, the Field of Blood, on 28 June 1119.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> At Ager Sanguinis, an army led by <a href="/wiki/Ilghazi" title="Ilghazi">Ilghazi</a> annihilated the Antiochian forces led by <a href="/wiki/Roger_of_Salerno" title="Roger of Salerno">Roger of Salerno</a> who was killed during the battle. The Muslim victory was short-lived, with Baldwin II and <a href="/wiki/Pons,_Count_of_Tripoli" title="Pons, Count of Tripoli">Pons of Tripoli</a> narrowly defeating Ilghazi's army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hab" title="Battle of Hab">Second Battle of Tell Danith</a> on 14 August 1119.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorton2018114–118The_Field_of_Blood:_The_battle_(1119)_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorton2018114–118The_Field_of_Blood:_The_battle_(1119)-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 16 January 1120, Baldwin II and the new patriarch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warmund,_Patriarch_of_Jerusalem" title="Warmund, Patriarch of Jerusalem">Warmund of Jerusalem</a> held the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Nablus" title="Council of Nablus">Council of Nablus</a>, establishing a rudimentary set of rules for governing the kingdom now known as the <a href="/wiki/Assizes_of_Jerusalem" title="Assizes of Jerusalem"><i>assizes</i> of Jerusalem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPrawer19803–84The_Feudal_System_and_Constitutional_History_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPrawer19803–84The_Feudal_System_and_Constitutional_History-58">[58]</a></sup> The formal establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">Knights Templar</a> was likely also granted by the council, complimenting the military arm of the <a href="/wiki/Knights_Hospitaller" title="Knights Hospitaller">Knights Hospitaller</a> that was protecting pilgrims to the Holy Land. Both military orders were accumulating holdings in the kingdom and Crusader states, with the Hospitallers eventually obtaining the famous <a href="/wiki/Krak_des_Chevaliers" title="Krak des Chevaliers">Krak des Chevaliers</a>, an important military and administrative center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELa_Monte19321–25The_Constitutional_Development_of_the_Latin_State_of_Jerusalem:_The_First_Kingdom,_1099–1174_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELa_Monte19321–25The_Constitutional_Development_of_the_Latin_State_of_Jerusalem:_The_First_Kingdom,_1099–1174-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Venetian_Crusade" title="Venetian Crusade">Venetian Crusade</a>, also known as the Crusade of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Callixtus_II" title="Pope Callixtus II">Calixtus II</a>, was conducted from 1122 to 1124.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The Western participants included those from the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Pons,_Count_of_Tripoli" title="Pons, Count of Tripoli">Pons of Tripoli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The actions resulted in the successful <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon#Crusader_period_(1124–1291)" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Siege of Tyre</a>, taking the city from the Damascene atabeg Toghtekin. This marked a major victor for Baldwin II prior to his <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_II_of_Jerusalem#Second_captivity" title="Baldwin II of Jerusalem">second captivity</a> in 1123.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952167–174Siege_of_Tyre,_Ransom_of_King_Baldwin_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952167–174Siege_of_Tyre,_Ransom_of_King_Baldwin-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1123, Baldwin II led a raid to <a href="/wiki/Suru%C3%A7" title="Suruç">Sarūj</a> in order to rescue hostages held by <a href="/wiki/Belek_Ghazi" title="Belek Ghazi">Belek Ghazi</a> and was also captured. Belek died in May 1124 and Baldwin II was seized by Ilghazi's son, <a href="/wiki/Husam_al-Din_Timurtash" title="Husam al-Din Timurtash">Timurtash</a>, who commenced negotiations for Baldwin's release. After a portion of the ransom was paid, additional hostages, to include Baldwin's youngest daughter <a href="/wiki/Ioveta_of_Bethany" title="Ioveta of Bethany">Jovetta</a>, were provided secure the payment of the balance, Baldwin II was released from the <a href="/wiki/Citadel_of_Aleppo" title="Citadel of Aleppo">Citadel of Aleppo</a> on 29 August 1124. Jovetta was held by <a href="/wiki/Aqsunqur_al-Bursuqi" title="Aqsunqur al-Bursuqi">il-Bursuqi</a> and were ransomed by Baldwin II in 1125 using his spoils from the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Azaz_(1125)" title="Battle of Azaz (1125)">Battle of Azaz</a> of 1125.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952161–166King_Baldwin_II_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952161–166King_Baldwin_II-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Toghtekin died in February 1128, and Baldwin II began the <a href="/wiki/Crusade_of_1129" title="Crusade of 1129">Crusade of 1129</a>, also known as the Damascus Crusade, shortly thereafter. The objective was Damascus, now led by the new atabeg <a href="/wiki/Taj_al-Muluk_Buri" title="Taj al-Muluk Buri">Taj al-Muluk Buri</a>, the son of Toghtekin. The Crusaders were able to capture the town of <a href="/wiki/Banias#Crusader/Ayyubid_period" title="Banias">Banias</a>, but were unable to take Damascus despite coming within six miles of the town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Baldwin II and Morphia married their eldest daughter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melisende_of_Jerusalem" title="Melisende of Jerusalem">Melisende of Jerusalem</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulk_V_of_Anjou" title="Fulk V of Anjou">Fulk V of Anjou</a> in 1129 in anticipation of a royal succession. Baldwin II fell ill in Antioch and died on 21 August 1131.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Fulk and Melisende were crowned joint rulers of Jerusalem on 14 September 1131 in the same church where Baldwin II had been laid to rest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Fulk assumed full control of the government, excluding Melisende, as he favored fellow Angevins to the native nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_rise_of_Zengi">The rise of Zengi</span></h4>
<p>At the same time, the advent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imad_ad-Din_Zengi" title="Imad ad-Din Zengi">Imad ad-Din Zengi</a> saw the Crusaders threatened by a Muslim ruler who would introduce <i><a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a></i> to the conflict, joining the powerful Syrian emirates in a combined effort against the Franks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> He became <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Mosul#Zengid_emirs" title="List of rulers of Mosul">atabeg of Mosul</a> in September 1127 and used this to expand his control to <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Aleppo#Zengid_Dynasty" title="List of rulers of Aleppo">Aleppo</a> in June 1128.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEl-Azhari201610–23The_Early_Career_of_Zengi,_1084–1127:_the_Turkmen_influence_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEl-Azhari201610–23The_Early_Career_of_Zengi,_1084–1127:_the_Turkmen_influence-69">[69]</a></sup> In 1135, Zengi moved against Antioch and, when the Crusaders failed to put an army into the field to oppose him, he captured several important Syrian town. He defeated Fulk at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ba%27rin" title="Battle of Ba'rin">Battle of Ba'rin</a> of 1137, seizing <a href="/wiki/Baarin#Medieval_period" title="Baarin">Ba'rin Castle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaalouf2006123–142An_Emir_among_Barbarians_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaalouf2006123–142An_Emir_among_Barbarians-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1137, Zengi invaded <a href="/wiki/County_of_Tripoli" title="County of Tripoli">Tripoli</a>, killing the count <a href="/wiki/Pons,_Count_of_Tripoli" title="Pons, Count of Tripoli">Pons of Tripoli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaalouf2006109–122The_Damascus_Conspiracies_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaalouf2006109–122The_Damascus_Conspiracies-71">[71]</a></sup> Fulk intervened, but Zengi's troops captured Pons' successor <a href="/wiki/Raymond_II,_Count_of_Tripoli" title="Raymond II, Count of Tripoli">Raymond II of Tripoli</a>, and besieged Fulk in the border castle of <a href="/wiki/Montferrand_(crusader_castle)" title="Montferrand (crusader castle)">Montferrand</a>. Fulk surrendered the castle and paid Zengi a ransom for his and Raymond's freedom. <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos" title="John II Komnenos">John II Komnenos</a>, emperor since 1118, reasserted Byzantine claims to <a href="/wiki/John_II_Komnenos#Campaigning_in_Cilicia_and_Syria_(1137–1138)" title="John II Komnenos">Cilicia and Antioch</a>, compelling <a href="/wiki/Raymond_of_Poitiers" title="Raymond of Poitiers">Raymond of Poitiers</a> to give homage. In April 1138, the Byzantines and Franks jointly besieged <a href="/wiki/Aleppo#Seljuq_and_Ayyubid_periods" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> and, with no success, began the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Shaizar" title="Siege of Shaizar">Siege of Shaizar</a>, abandoning it a month later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952214–216The_Christians_lay_siege_to_Shaizar_(1138)_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952214–216The_Christians_lay_siege_to_Shaizar_(1138)-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 13 November 1143, while the royal couple were in Acre, Fulk was killed in a hunting accident. On Christmas Day 1143, their son <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_III_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin III of Jerusalem">Baldwin III of Jerusalem</a> was crowned co-king with his mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> That same year, having prepared his army for a renewed attack on Antioch, John II Komnenos went hunting wild boar, cutting himself with a poisoned arrow. He died on 8 April 1143 and was succeeded as emperor by his son <a href="/wiki/Manuel_I_Komnenos" title="Manuel I Komnenos">Manuel I Komnenos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-images.library.wisc.edu_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-images.library.wisc.edu-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following John's death, the Byzantine army withdrew, leaving Zengi unopposed. Fulk's death later in the year left <a href="/wiki/Joscelin_II,_Count_of_Edessa" title="Joscelin II, Count of Edessa">Joscelin II of Edessa</a> with no powerful allies to help defend Edessa. Zengi came north to begin the first <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Edessa_(1144)" title="Siege of Edessa (1144)">Siege of Edessa</a>, arriving on 28 November 1144.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The city had been warned of his arrival and was prepared for a siege, but there was little they could do. Zengi realized there was no defending force and surrounded the city. The walls collapsed on 24 December 1144. Zengi's troops rushed into the city, killing all those who were unable to flee. All the Frankish prisoners were executed, but the native Christians were allowed to live. The Crusaders were dealt their first major defeat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952225–246The_Fall_of_Edessa_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952225–246The_Fall_of_Edessa-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Zengi was assassinated by a slave on 14 September 1146 and was succeeded in the <a href="/wiki/Zengid_dynasty" title="Zengid dynasty">Zengid dynasty</a> by his son <a href="/wiki/Nur_ad-Din_(died_1174)" title="Nur ad-Din (died 1174)">Nūr-ad-Din</a>. The Franks recaptured the city during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Edessa_(1146)" title="Siege of Edessa (1146)">Second Siege of Edessa</a> of 1146 by stealth but could not take or even properly besiege the citadel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> After a brief counter-siege, Nūr-ad-Din took the city. The population was massacred, with the women and children enslaved, and the walls razed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2012225–232Zangi—Champion_of_Islam,_The_Advent_of_Nūr-ad-Din_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2012225–232Zangi—Champion_of_Islam,_The_Advent_of_Nūr-ad-Din-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Crusade">Second Crusade</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">Second Crusade</a></div>
<p>The fall of Edessa caused great consternation in Jerusalem and Western Europe, tampering the enthusiastic success of the First Crusade. Calls for a new crusade—the <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">Second Crusade</a>—were immediate, and was the first to be led by European kings. Concurrent campaigns as part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a> are also sometimes associated with this Crusade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-79">[79]</a></sup> The aftermath of the Crusade saw the Muslim world united around <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a>, leading to the fall of Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Second_Crusade">The Second Crusade</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Eugene_III" title="Pope Eugene III">Eugene III</a>, recently elected pope, issued the bull <i><a href="/wiki/Quantum_praedecessores" title="Quantum praedecessores">Quantum praedecessores</a></i> in December 1145 calling for a new crusade, one that would be more organized and centrally controlled than the First. The armies would be led by the strongest kings of Europe and a route that would be pre-planned. The pope called on <a href="/wiki/Bernard_of_Clairvaux" title="Bernard of Clairvaux">Bernard of Clairvaux</a> to preach the Second Crusade, granting the same indulgences which had accorded to the First Crusaders. Among those answering the call were by two European kings, <a href="/wiki/Louis_VII_of_France" title="Louis VII of France">Louis VII of France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conrad_III_of_Germany" title="Conrad III of Germany">Conrad III of Germany</a>. Louis, his wife, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Aquitaine" title="Eleanor of Aquitaine">Eleanor of Aquitaine</a>, and many princes and lords prostrated themselves at the feet of Bernard in order to take the cross. Conrad and his nephew <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick Barbarossa</a> also received the cross from the hand of Bernard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conrad III and the German contingent planned to leave for the Holy Land at Easter, but did not depart until May 1147. When the German army began to cross Byzantine territory, emperor Manuel I had his troops posted to ensure against trouble. A brief <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Constantinople_(1147)" title="Battle of Constantinople (1147)">Battle of Constantinople</a> in September ensued, and their defeat at the emperor's hand convinced the Germans to move quickly to Asia Minor. Without waiting for the French contingent, Conrad III engaged the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum" title="Sultanate of Rum">Seljuks of Rûm</a> under sultan <a href="/wiki/Mesud_I" title="Mesud I">Mesud I</a>, son and successor of <a href="/wiki/Kilij_Arslan_I" title="Kilij Arslan I">Kilij Arslan</a>, the nemesis of the First Crusade. Mesud and his forces almost totally destroyed Conrad's contingent at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dorylaeum_(1147)" title="Battle of Dorylaeum (1147)">Second Battle of Dorylaeum</a> on 25 October 1147.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyerman2006268–303God's_Bargain:_Summoning_the_Second_Crusade_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyerman2006268–303God's_Bargain:_Summoning_the_Second_Crusade-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The French contingent departed in June 1147. In the meantime, <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">Roger II of Sicily</a>, an enemy of Conrad's, had invaded Byzantine territory. Manuel I needed all his army to counter this force, and, unlike the armies of the First Crusade, the Germans and French entered Asia with no Byzantine assistance. The French met the remnants of Conrad's army in northern Turkey, and Conrad joined Louis's force. They fended off a Seljuk attack at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ephesus_(1147)" title="Battle of Ephesus (1147)">Battle of Ephesus</a> on 24 December 1147. A few days later, they were again victorious at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Meander" title="Battle of the Meander">Battle of the Meander</a>. Louis was not as lucky at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mount_Cadmus" title="Battle of Mount Cadmus">Battle of Mount Cadmus</a> on 6 January 1148 when the army of Mesud inflicted heavy losses on the Crusaders. Shortly thereafter, they sailed for Antioch, almost totally destroyed by battle and sickness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952268–274The_French_in_Asia_Minor,_1147–1148_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952268–274The_French_in_Asia_Minor,_1147–1148-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Crusader army arrived at Antioch on 19 March 1148 with the intent on moving to retake Edessa, but Baldwin III of Jerusalem and the Knights Templar had other ideas. The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Acre" title="Council of Acre">Council of Acre</a> was held on 24 June 1148, changing the objective of the Second Crusade to Damascus, a former ally of the kingdom that had shifted its allegiance to that of the Zengids. The Crusaders fought the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosra_(1147)" title="Battle of Bosra (1147)">Battle of Bosra</a> with the Damascenes in the summer of 1147, with no clear winner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Bad luck and poor tactics of the Crusaders led to the disastrous five-day <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Damascus_(1148)" title="Siege of Damascus (1148)">Siege of Damascus</a> from 24 to 28 July 1148.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaalouf2006143–158Nūr-ad-Din,_the_Saint_King_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaalouf2006143–158Nūr-ad-Din,_the_Saint_King-85">[85]</a></sup> The barons of Jerusalem withdrew support and the Crusaders retreated before the arrival of a relief army led by Nūr-ad-Din. Morale fell, hostility to the Byzantines grew and distrust developed between the newly arrived Crusaders and those that had made the region their home after the earlier crusades. The French and German forces felt betrayed by the other, lingering for a generation due to the defeat, to the ruin of the Christian kingdoms in the Holy Land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952278–288Fiasco_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952278–288Fiasco-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the spring of 1147, Eugene III authorized the expansion of his mission into the Iberian peninsula, equating these campaigns against the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> with the rest of the Second Crusade. The successful <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Lisbon" title="Siege of Lisbon">Siege of Lisbon</a>, from 1 July to 25 October 1147, was followed by the six-month <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tortosa_(1148)" title="Siege of Tortosa (1148)">Siege of Tortosa</a>, ending on 30 December 1148 with a defeat for the Moors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In the north, some Germans were reluctant to fight in the Holy Land while the pagan <a href="/wiki/Wends" title="Wends">Wends</a> were a more immediate problem. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Wendish_Crusade" title="Wendish Crusade">Wendish Crusade</a> of 1147 was partially successful but failed to convert the pagans to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The disastrous performance of this campaign in the Holy Land damaged the standing of the papacy, soured relations between the Christians of the kingdom and the West for many years, and encouraged the Muslims of Syria to even greater efforts to defeat the Franks. The dismal failures of this Crusade then set the stage for the fall of Jerusalem, leading to the Third Crusade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952278–288Fiasco_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952278–288Fiasco-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="N.C5.ABr-ad-Din_and_the_rise_of_Saladin"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nūr-ad-Din_and_the_rise_of_Saladin">Nūr-ad-Din and the rise of Saladin</span></h4>
<p>In the first major encounter after the Second Crusade, Nūr-ad-Din's forces then destroyed the Crusader army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Inab" title="Battle of Inab">Battle of Inab</a> on 29 June 1149. <a href="/wiki/Raymond_of_Poitiers" title="Raymond of Poitiers">Raymond of Poitiers</a>, as prince of Antioch, came to the aid of the besieged city. Raymond was killed and his head was presented to Nūr-ad-Din, who forwarded it to the caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Muqtafi" title="Al-Muqtafi">al-Muqtafi</a> in Baghdad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In 1150, Nūr-ad-Din defeated <a href="/wiki/Joscelin_II,_Count_of_Edessa" title="Joscelin II, Count of Edessa">Joscelin II of Edessa</a> for a final time, resulting in Joscelin being publicly blinded, dying in prison in Aleppo in 1159. Later that year, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aintab" title="Battle of Aintab">Battle of Aintab</a>, he tried but failed to prevent Baldwin III's evacuation of the residents of <a href="/wiki/Turbessel#Byzantine_Period" title="Turbessel">Turbessel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952329–330Turbessel_ceded_to_Byzantium_(1150)_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952329–330Turbessel_ceded_to_Byzantium_(1150)-90">[90]</a></sup> The unconquered portions of the County of Edessa would nevertheless fall to the Zengids within a few years. In 1152, <a href="/wiki/Raymond_II,_Count_of_Tripoli" title="Raymond II, Count of Tripoli">Raymond II of Tripoli</a> became the first Frankish victim of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Assassins" title="Order of Assassins">Assassins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis2017167Military_Decline_and_Matrimonial_Discord:_Count_Raymond_II_(1137–1152)_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis2017167Military_Decline_and_Matrimonial_Discord:_Count_Raymond_II_(1137–1152)-91">[91]</a></sup> Later that year, Nūr-ad-Din captured and burned <a href="/wiki/Tartus#Crusades" title="Tartus">Tortosa</a>, briefly occupying the town, before it was taken by the Knights Templar as a military headquarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952333Murder_of_Raymond_II_(1152)_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952333Murder_of_Raymond_II_(1152)-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BattleOfInab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1002" data-file-width="1098" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/BattleOfInab.jpg/220px-BattleOfInab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/BattleOfInab.jpg/330px-BattleOfInab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/BattleOfInab.jpg/440px-BattleOfInab.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BattleOfInab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nur_ad-Din_(died_1174)" title="Nur ad-Din (died 1174)">Nūr-ad-Din's</a> victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Inab" title="Battle of Inab">Battle of Inab</a>, 1149</div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Ascalon" title="Siege of Ascalon">Siege of Ascalon</a> ended on 22 August 1153 with a Crusader victory and Damascus was taken by Nūr-ad-Din the next year, uniting all of Syria under Zengid rule. In 1156, Baldwin III was forced into a treaty with Nūr-ad-Din, and later entered into an alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_III_of_Jerusalem#Byzantine_alliance" title="Baldwin III of Jerusalem">Byzantine Empire</a>. On 18 May 1157, Nūr-ad-Din began a siege on the Knights Hospitaller contingent at <a href="/wiki/Banias#Crusader/Ayyubid_period" title="Banias">Banias</a>, with the Grand Master <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bertrand_de_Blanquefort" title="Bertrand de Blanquefort">Bertrand de Blanquefort</a> captured. Baldwin III was able to break the siege, only to be ambushed at <a href="/wiki/Daughters_of_Jacob_Bridge#Crusader_and_Ayyubid_period" title="Daughters of Jacob Bridge">Jacob's Ford</a> in June. Reinforcements from Antioch and Tripoli were able to relieve the besieged Crusaders. Bertrand's captivity lasted until 1159, when emperor Manuel I negotiated an alliance with Nūr-ad-Din against the Seljuks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952338–342The_Rise_of_Nur_ed-Din:_The_Capture_of_Ascalon,_1153_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952338–342The_Rise_of_Nur_ed-Din:_The_Capture_of_Ascalon,_1153-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Baldwin III died on 10 February 1163, and <a href="/wiki/Amalric_of_Jerusalem" title="Amalric of Jerusalem">Amalric of Jerusalem</a> was crowned as king of Jerusalem eight days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> He undertook a series of four <a href="/wiki/Crusader_invasions_of_Egypt" title="Crusader invasions of Egypt">invasions of Egypt</a> from 1163 to 1169, taking advantage of weaknesses of the Fatimids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-images.library.wisc.edu_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-images.library.wisc.edu-74">[74]</a></sup> Nūr-ad-Din's intervention in the first invasion allowed his general <a href="/wiki/Shirkuh" title="Shirkuh">Shirkuh</a>, accompanied by his nephew <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a>, to enter Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shawar" title="Shawar">Shawar</a>, the deposed vizier to the Fatimid caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Adid" title="Al-Adid">al-Adid</a>, allied with Amalric I, attacking Shirkuh at the second <a href="/wiki/Bilbeis" title="Bilbeis">Siege of Bilbeis</a> beginning in August 1164, following Amalric's unsuccessful first siege in September 1163.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERunciman1952380–382Amalric_advances_on_Cairo_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERunciman1952380–382Amalric_advances_on_Cairo-96">[96]</a></sup> This action left the Holy Land lacking in defenses, and Nūr-ad-Din defeated a Crusader forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Harim" title="Battle of Harim">Battle of Harim</a> in August 1164, capturing most of the Franks' leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber2012240The_Zengid_Threat_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber2012240The_Zengid_Threat-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the sacking of Bilbeis, the Crusader-Egyptian force was to meet Shirkuh's army in the indecisive <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_al-Babein" title="Battle of al-Babein">Battle of al-Babein</a> on 18 March 1167. In 1169, both Shawar and Shirkuh died, and al-Adid appointed Saladin as vizier. Saladin, with reinforcements from Nūr-ad-Din, defeated a massive Crusader-Byzantine force at the <a href="/wiki/Crusader_invasions_of_Egypt#Siege_of_Damietta" title="Crusader invasions of Egypt">Siege of Damietta</a> in late October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> This gained Saladin the attention of the Assassins, with attempts on his life in January 1175 and again on 22 May 1176.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis2003113–117The_Old_Man_of_the_Mountain_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis2003113–117The_Old_Man_of_the_Mountain-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Baldwin_IV_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin IV of Jerusalem">Baldwin IV of Jerusalem</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> became king on 5 July 1174 at the age of 13.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-101">[101]</a></sup> As a leper he was not expected to live long, and served with a number of regents, and served as co-ruler with his cousin <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_V_of_Jerusalem" title="Baldwin V of Jerusalem">Baldwin V of Jerusalem</a> beginning in 1183. Baldwin IV, <a href="/wiki/Raynald_of_Ch%C3%A2tillon" title="Raynald of Châtillon">Raynald of Châtillon</a> and the Knights Templar defeated Saladin at the celebrated <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Montgisard" title="Battle of Montgisard">Battle of Montgisard</a> on 25 November 1177. In June 1179, the Crusaders were defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marj_Ayyun" title="Battle of Marj Ayyun">Battle of Marj Ayyub</a>, and in August the unfinished castle at <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jacob%27s_Ford" title="Siege of Jacob's Ford">Jacob's Ford</a> fell to Saladin, with the slaughter of half its Templar garrison. However, the kingdom repelled his attacks at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Belvoir_Castle_(1182)" title="Battle of Belvoir Castle (1182)">Battle of Belvoir Castle</a> in 1182 and later in the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Kerak" title="Siege of Kerak">Siege of Kerak</a> of 1183.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_fall_of_Jerusalem">The fall of Jerusalem</span></h4>
<p>Baldwin V became sole king upon the death of his uncle in 1185 under the regency of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raymond_III_of_Tripoli" title="Raymond III of Tripoli">Raymond III of Tripoli</a>. Raymond negotiated a truce with Saladin which went awry when the king died in the summer of 1186.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> His mother <a href="/wiki/Sibylla,_Queen_of_Jerusalem" title="Sibylla, Queen of Jerusalem">Sibylla of Jerusalem</a> and her husband <a href="/wiki/Guy_of_Lusignan" title="Guy of Lusignan">Guy of Lusignan</a> were crowned as queen and king of Jerusalem in the summer of 1186, shortly thereafter. They immediately had to deal with the threat posed by Saladin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite his defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_al-Fule" title="Battle of al-Fule">Battle of al-Fule</a> in the fall of 1183, Saladin increased his attacks against the Franks, leading to their defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cresson" title="Battle of Cresson">Battle of Cresson</a> on 1 May 1187. Guy of Lusignan responded by raising the largest army that Jerusalem had ever put into the field. Saladin lured this force into inhospitable terrain without water supplies and routed them at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hattin" title="Battle of Hattin">Battle of Hattin</a> on 4 July 1187. One of the major commanders was <a href="/wiki/Raymond_III,_Count_of_Tripoli#Hattin_and_its_consequences" title="Raymond III, Count of Tripoli">Raymond III of Tripoli</a> who saw his force slaughtered, with some knights deserting to the enemy, and narrowly escaping, only to be regarded as a traitor and coward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis2017233–284The_Regent_Thwarted:_Count_Raymond_III_(1174–1187)_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis2017233–284The_Regent_Thwarted:_Count_Raymond_III_(1174–1187)-105">[105]</a></sup> Guy of Lusignan was one of the few captives of Saladin's after the battle, along with Raynald of Châtillon and <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_IV_of_Toron" title="Humphrey IV of Toron">Humphrey IV of Toron</a>. Raynald was beheaded, settling an old score. Guy and Humphrey were imprisoned in Damascus and later released in 1188.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of his victory, much of Palestine quickly fell to Saladin. The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(1187)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (1187)">Siege of Jerusalem</a> began on 20 September 1187 and the Holy City was surrendered to Saladin by <a href="/wiki/Balian_of_Ibelin" title="Balian of Ibelin">Balian of Ibelin</a> on 2 October. According to some, on 19<span class="nowrap"> </span>October 1187, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_III" title="Pope Urban III">Urban III</a> died upon of hearing of the defeat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2012367Called_to_Crusade_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsbridge2012367Called_to_Crusade-107">[107]</a></sup> Jerusalem was once again in Muslim hands. Many in the kingdom fled to Tyre, and Saladin's subsequent attack at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tyre_(1187)" title="Siege of Tyre (1187)">Siege of Tyre</a> beginning in November 1187 was unsuccessful. The later <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Safed_(1188)" title="Siege of Safed (1188)">Siege of Safed</a> in late 1188 completed Saladin's conquest of the Holy Land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-:22-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Crusader_states_1190-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1330" data-file-width="978" decoding="async" height="408" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Map_Crusader_states_1190-en.svg/300px-Map_Crusader_states_1190-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Map_Crusader_states_1190-en.svg/450px-Map_Crusader_states_1190-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Map_Crusader_states_1190-en.svg/600px-Map_Crusader_states_1190-en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crusader_States_1190.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Near East, c. 1190, at the inception of the Third Crusade</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_Crusade">Third Crusade</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third Crusade</a></div>
<p>The years following the founding of the Kingdom of Jerusalem were met with multiple disasters.
